Editorial

Jim Dratwa, Head of the EGE Secretariat

In his State of the Union address on 11 September 2013, President Barroso underscored the importance 
of values — European values and values that are shared and upheld internationally.

He called upon us to look towards the future with a wisdom we gained from the past: ‘The European 
ideal touches the very foundations of European society. It is about values.’ In today’s world, the suprana-
tional level at which the European Union is placed is ‘indispensable to protect these values and standards 
and promote citizens’ rights’.

Last year’s Nobel Peace Prize reminded us of that historic achievement: that Europe is a project of peace, 
a project founded on and sustained by values, such as the rule of law and the values enshrined in the 
Charter of Fundamental Rights.

In distinct and crucial ways, both the European Commission International Dialogue on Bioethics and the 
European Group on Ethics in Science and New Technologies (EGE) play an invaluable part in this regard.

On 22 March 2011, the President of the European Commission requested that the EGE issue an opinion 
on the ethical implications of information and communication technology (ICT).

Following the 2011 Competitiveness Council meeting on the Euratom programme, the Commission also 
asked the EGE to contribute to the debate on a sustainable energy mix in Europe by issuing an opinion 
on the ethical impact of energy choices.

In 2013, having issued the above opinions and in accordance with the request from the President of the 
European Commission, the EGE will develop its opinion on security and surveillance technologies.

On its own initiative, the EGE has also adopted its position on the proposal for a regulation of the Euro-
pean Parliament and the Council on clinical trials. The European Group on Ethics’ position has since 
been appreciatively acknowledged and drawn upon in the interinstitutional process of maturation of the 
clinical trials regulation.

With the entry into force of the Lisbon Treaty, ethical considerations have risen in prominence and are 
an integral component in the formulation of European policies. While the need to continue to attend to 
core bioethical issues is undiminished, there is also an increasing demand to embed this societal–ethical 
attention in further key policy areas.

At European level as well as internationally, giving due consideration to all implications of emerging 
socio-technical  developments,  i.e.  to  science  and  new  technologies  broadly  construed,  is  ever  more 
important. So too is the need to develop a robust and sustained international dialogue in this area.

Brussels, 11 September 2013

Dr Jim Dratwa
Member of the Bureau of European Policy Advisers (BEPA)
Head of the EGE Secretariat
Head of the BEPA Ethics Sector

Ethically speaking 18, September 2013

German Ethics Council

Recent opinion on the future of genetic diagnosis

In  response  to  a  commission  by  the  federal  government,  in  April  2013  the  German  Ethics  Council 
compiled  an  opinion  on  the  future  of  genetic  diagnosis.  On  the  basis  of  the  right  of  people  affected 
by a genetic test to know, their right not to know and their right of informational self-determination, 
the council recommends in particular measures for the improvement of information, explanation and 
counselling. Other proposed requirements include special provisions on the conduct of prenatal genetic 
diagnosis, protection of those lacking the capacity to give consent, the quality of genetic tests and their 
funding within the healthcare system. The council not only offers recommendations for political and 
legislative action, but also aspires to stimulate public debate on future developments in genetic diagnosis.

Owing to falling costs, faster analyses and the advent of direct-to-consumer genetic testing via the Inter-
net, more and more people now have access to genetic diagnosis. In addition, it is becoming ever easier 
to obtain comprehensive genetic information about an individual or group of individuals, to the extent 
even of complete genome sequencing. In the case of an unborn child, genetic diagnosis without the iatro-
genic risk of miscarriage is possible by means of tests on the mother’s blood.

(cid:41)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:74)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:73)(cid:3)(cid:71)(cid:76)(cid:73)(cid:73)(cid:72)(cid:85)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:74)(cid:3)(cid:86)(cid:76)(cid:74)(cid:81)(cid:76)(cid:191)(cid:70)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:70)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:71)(cid:72)(cid:85)(cid:76)(cid:89)(cid:72)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:73)(cid:85)(cid:82)(cid:80)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:79)(cid:68)(cid:85)(cid:74)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:89)(cid:82)(cid:79)(cid:88)(cid:80)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:73)(cid:3)(cid:74)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:72)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:3)(cid:71)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:68)(cid:3)(cid:69)(cid:92)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:3)(cid:89)(cid:68)(cid:85)(cid:76)(cid:72)(cid:87)(cid:92)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:73)(cid:3)(cid:72)(cid:89)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:88)-
ations. Some of these are highly valuable for healthcare purposes, others present disturbing information 
on untreatable conditions, while the relevance of still others is unclear. The risk of misinterpretation and 
misunderstanding is substantial if genetic diagnosis is not offered and conducted at a high level of qual-
ity and with due allowance also for non-genetic factors.

In  this  situation,  the  council  suggests  various  measures  for  the  protection  of  self-determination  and 
health and for the preservation of fairness and solidarity in both the healthcare system and society at 
large.

provision of information to the public and in the training, advanced training and continuing training of 
(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:70)(cid:68)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:85)(cid:82)(cid:73)(cid:72)(cid:86)(cid:86)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:79)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:89)(cid:68)(cid:76)(cid:79)(cid:68)(cid:69)(cid:79)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:74)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:72)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:72)(cid:86)(cid:87)(cid:86)(cid:15)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:76)(cid:85)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:80)(cid:83)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:87)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:70)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:86)(cid:76)(cid:74)(cid:81)(cid:76)(cid:191)(cid:70)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:70)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:73)(cid:3)
their results.

Secondly, the council recommends a number of amendments to the Genetic Diagnosis Act in order to 
guarantee  high  standards  of  explanation  and  counselling  in  the  light  of  recent  developments.  In  this 
(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:74)(cid:68)(cid:85)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:71)(cid:71)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:86)(cid:86)(cid:72)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:80)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:87)(cid:72)(cid:85)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:86)(cid:88)(cid:70)(cid:75)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:72)(cid:91)(cid:70)(cid:72)(cid:86)(cid:86)(cid:76)(cid:89)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:73)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:80)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:86)(cid:72)(cid:70)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:68)(cid:85)(cid:92)(cid:3)(cid:191)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:74)(cid:86)(cid:15)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:73)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:80)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:73)(cid:68)(cid:80)(cid:76)(cid:79)(cid:92)(cid:3)
members and the interests of people who lack the capacity to give consent. In particular, the council’s 
majority recommendation is that in future medical explanation and counselling should be mandatory 
even in the case of genetic tests conducted for non-medical purposes, as such tests too may yield medi-
cally relevant results.

Thirdly, to mitigate the risks of direct-to-consumer genetic tests and the possible psychological burden 
of their results, the council calls for improved, EU-wide measures to provide for independent consumer 
information and for patient and consumer protection.

In addition, the council presents recommendations on whether and to what extent the healthcare system 
should cover the cost of diagnostic procedures used in the context of therapy, on technical quality assur-
ance, on the funding of research and on research and health policy.

go ahead with the birth of a disabled child should be highly valued in society, and calls for more relief for 
affected families. The council considers it essential that the conduct of prenatal genetic tests should be 
conditional upon subsequent ultrasound examinations carried out for the purpose of differentiation and 

10 

Ethically speaking 18, September 2013

upon independent psychosocial counselling. It further recommends that funds be allocated to concomi-
tant socio-empirical and ethical research on prenatal genetic diagnosis.

The majority of the members of the German Ethics Council in addition consider that prenatal genetic 
diagnosis should be permitted only where an increased risk of a disorder of genetic origin exists. In their 
view it is essential to ensure that no genetic information concerning the unborn child that is unconnected 
with pathology and no details of possible carrier status that are irrelevant to the health of the child itself 
are disclosed.

(cid:54)(cid:75)(cid:82)(cid:88)(cid:79)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:74)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:72)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:73)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:80)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:88)(cid:81)(cid:69)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:70)(cid:75)(cid:76)(cid:79)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:68)(cid:71)(cid:92)(cid:3)(cid:69)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:89)(cid:68)(cid:76)(cid:79)(cid:68)(cid:69)(cid:79)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:71)(cid:88)(cid:85)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:74)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:74)(cid:81)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:70)(cid:92)(cid:3)(cid:90)(cid:76)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:191)(cid:85)(cid:86)(cid:87)(cid:3)
12 weeks of fertilisation, the majority of the members of the council further consider mandatory pre-ter-
mination counselling pursuant to Section 218a(1) of the Penal Code (the ‘counselling solution’) to be 
inadequate and call for the introduction of a more extensive system of protection.

This view is rejected by eight members of the council, who propose in a dissenting position statement 
that an expectant mother should not experience obstacles in gaining access to genetic information about 
her unborn child if she considers this to be essential in enabling her to reach a responsible decision. For 
this reason, these members oppose the suggested restrictions on prenatal genetic diagnosis. In addition, 
they recommend an amendment to the Genetic Diagnosis Act that would in future also allow the unborn 
child to be examined for late-onset disorders.

In another dissenting position statement, four council members express the view that non-invasive pre-

Current work

Activities

Annual meetings

23 May 2013: Pharmaceutical research, but at whose expense?

How can participants in pharmaceutical studies be protected and ethical standards be effectively enforced 
in the global context? How can research quality be ensured in a global context? Are ethics commit-
tees suited to and necessary for the protection of test subjects? What ethical standards should European 
research projects take into account? It was to questions such as these and others that the German Ethics 
Council sought answers at its 2013 annual meeting in Berlin, in the course of which it held discussions 
with around 300 delegates.

The  evening’s  contributions  are  accessible  online  as  both  audio  and  text  ((cid:75)(cid:87)(cid:87)(cid:83)(cid:29)(cid:18)(cid:18)(cid:90)(cid:90)(cid:90)(cid:17)(cid:72)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:76)(cid:78)(cid:85)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:17)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:74)(cid:18)

Bioethics forum

24 April 2013: Solidarity under conditions of demographic change

What is the responsibility of the individual and society for shaping demographic change? How can soli-
darity and tolerance be encouraged? These questions were debated by the German Ethics Council with 
Federal Chancellor Angela Merkel and over 350 guests at a meeting in the council’s ‘Bioethics forum’ 
series.

Remit of NEC
The Hellenic National Bioethics Commission is an independent advisory body of experts that 
examines ethical questions raised by advances in the biological sciences and issues recommenda-
tions addressed to the relevant public authorities. The commission functions under the umbrella 
of the general secretariat of the government and its mission is to highlight the interaction of life

Hellenic National Bioethics Commission

A. Most recently issued opinion (April, 2013)

The commission has recently issued an opinion on ‘Enhancement of human characteristics: physical 
characteristics’ (translation in English still pending).

Abstract

The  Hellenic  National  Bioethics  Commission  dealt  with  the  issue  of  human  enhancement.  The  term 
includes the use of medical interventions in healthy subjects in order to obtain desirable physical, mental

Enhancement of physical abilities

improving physical abilities is, in principle, a legitimate choice. However, this choice is subject to restric-
(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:86)(cid:15)(cid:3)(cid:90)(cid:75)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:75)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:79)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:72)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:69)(cid:82)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:72)(cid:85)(cid:86)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:75)(cid:76)(cid:80)(cid:18)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:85)(cid:86)(cid:72)(cid:79)(cid:73)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:76)(cid:85)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:68)(cid:85)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:72)(cid:86)(cid:17)

Protecting a person’s health from possibly damaging agents is imperative. According to the commis-
sion, it is important that the competent authorities implement strict control procedures over the products 
(cid:88)(cid:86)(cid:72)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:73)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:75)(cid:92)(cid:86)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:3)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:75)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:70)(cid:72)(cid:80)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:87)(cid:15)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:68)(cid:85)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:88)(cid:79)(cid:68)(cid:85)(cid:79)(cid:92)(cid:3)(cid:86)(cid:76)(cid:74)(cid:81)(cid:76)(cid:191)(cid:70)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:73)(cid:88)(cid:79)(cid:79)(cid:92)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:70)(cid:70)(cid:88)(cid:85)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:72)(cid:79)(cid:92)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:73)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:80)(cid:3)(cid:70)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:86)(cid:88)(cid:80)-
ers about the possible side effects of agents that do not require a prescription. Regarding agents that are 

14 

Ethically speaking 18, September 2013

administrated after encouragement (by sports medicine physicians, trainers, etc.), the relevant responsi-
bility — moral but also legal — belongs primarily to the person recommending the use.

In the case of enhancing abilities through doping in order to participate in athletic events, the commis-
sion notes the need to strengthen the role of the Hellenic National Anti-Doping Agency. Additionally, 
the commission considers that reinforcing athletes’ medical assistance will act as a deterrent to the use 
of doping, and emphasises that it is important to responsibly publish the results of doping control tests to 
avoid the risk of stigmatising sports.

(Please note that since the English version of the recently renewed website is still under construction, 
the full text (in PDF format) of all opinions and reports issued by the commission in English can be 
requested from the secretariat (Secretariat@bioethics.gr).)

B. Current work

Currently the commission is discussing ‘Enhancement of human characteristics: mental and cognitive 
characteristics’.

This initiative, which is addressed mainly at young scientists (lawyers, physicians, sociologists) aims

E. Communicative tools

Since October 2008 the commission’s newsletter ‘Bio-Ethica’ has proved to be a most valuable tool for 
communication  of  the  commission  with  the  general  public.  The  newsletter  includes  announcements, 
news and events of the commission, international news related to bioethics, book reviews and informa-
tion on local and international bioethics meetings or conferences, and is sent to an extensive list of recipi-
ents in Greece at the end of every month.

(cid:135)(cid:3) General Estates on the End of Life from November to December 2013: 

This autumn, the NCEC organizes General Estates on the end of life as part of the new mis-
sions to which it has been entrusted by the 2011 bioethics law in terms of public debate. A sam-
ple group of twenty citizens, representative of the French diversity, will be selected to par-
(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:76)(cid:83)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:71)(cid:88)(cid:85)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:74)(cid:3)(cid:73)(cid:82)(cid:88)(cid:85)(cid:3)(cid:90)(cid:72)(cid:72)(cid:78)(cid:16)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:70)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:73)(cid:72)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:70)(cid:72)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:71)(cid:72)(cid:69)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:72)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:16)(cid:82)(cid:73)(cid:16)(cid:79)(cid:76)(cid:73)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:86)(cid:86)(cid:88)(cid:72)(cid:86)(cid:17)(cid:3)(cid:55)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:191)(cid:85)(cid:86)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:90)(cid:82)(cid:3)
week-ends, they will hear speakers proposed by the NCEC, who will present them different and 
sometimes opposite outlooks over different questions. The third week-end, they will choose 
themselves the people they want to listen, or to question more precisely. Finally, on the fourth 
week-end, they will return their opinion or recommendations on the approached issues. Their 
opinion will be presented in a press conference the day after the end of the General Estates. 

Ethically speaking 18, September 2013

ITALY

NEC name: National Bioethics Committee

1.

2.

Year of establishment of NEC 
The National Bioethics Committee (NBC) was established by a decree signed by the President of 
the Council of Ministers on 28 March 1990.

Remit of NEC
The NBC is an interdisciplinary and pluralistic body that:

(a) provides ethics consultation to the government, the parliament and other national and interna-
tional institutions in the preparation of regulatory frameworks;

(cid:11)(cid:69)(cid:12)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:73)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:80)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:88)(cid:69)(cid:79)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:83)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:69)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:72)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:86)(cid:86)(cid:88)(cid:72)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:86)(cid:88)(cid:79)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:74)(cid:3)(cid:73)(cid:85)(cid:82)(cid:80)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:72)(cid:70)(cid:75)(cid:81)(cid:82)(cid:79)(cid:82)(cid:74)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:86)(cid:70)(cid:76)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:191)(cid:70)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:85)(cid:82)(cid:74)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:86)(cid:86)(cid:3)
in biomedical and social areas;

(c) establishes and maintains relations at European and international levels.

Ethically speaking 18, September 2013

Update on the National Bioethics Committee’s activities in 2013

Opinions

(cid:135)(cid:3) Human rights, medical ethics and enhancement technologies in military contexts (13 March 2013)

In an ever-uncertain world, yet characterised by a unique convergence of interests and coopera-
tion agreements between industry, research institutes and military institutions, the hypothesis of 
an ever-more engineered soldier far from the ordinary citizen comes forward. A soldier to whom 
enhancing technologies are applied, that is, ‘strategies to create human capabilities that go beyond 
normal  biological  variability,  through  changes  in  human  function’,  including  surgery,  genetic 
(cid:80)(cid:82)(cid:71)(cid:76)(cid:191)(cid:70)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:86)(cid:15)(cid:3)(cid:81)(cid:72)(cid:88)(cid:85)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:3)(cid:86)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:80)(cid:88)(cid:79)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:15)(cid:3)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:75)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:70)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:74)(cid:3)(cid:71)(cid:85)(cid:88)(cid:74)(cid:86)(cid:17)

The committee, assuming the shared grounds of the repudiation of war, expresses a general judge-
ment of ethical disvalue on the particular topic of enhancement technologies in the military. Many 
of  these  technologies  represent  a  risk  that  goes  beyond  the  limits  set  by  international  laws  as 
regards to military activities and war, both for the individuals, civilians and military, who are sub-
ject to them, and for the opponents.

The committee is aware that technological evolution on the one hand and military needs on the 
other will lead to the adoption of some of these technologies. Concerned by these developments, 
the committee considers necessary the establishment of a strong conviction that some fundamen-

of safer and more effective cognitive enhancers than those currently available, while emphasising 
the many bioethical and policy issues which should also be discussed and addressed. The com-

the problems of health justice, recommends society should be adequately informed on the risks of 
these drugs and fosters a public discussion on the more general issues of cognitive enhancement. 
The committee also points out how cognitive functions may be improved in a more lasting man-
ner, respecting personal growth through study and the continuous stimulation of interest, as well 
as through a rich social life, relationships and healthy lifestyle (nutrition, physical activity), and 
highlights how overestimated expectations of the enhancing effects of pharmacological neuro-
stimulants stem from a short-sighted vision of human intelligence.

(cid:135)(cid:3) (cid:39)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:73)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:70)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:83)(cid:86)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:73)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:3)(cid:86)(cid:70)(cid:76)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:191)(cid:70)(cid:3)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:86)(cid:72)(cid:68)(cid:85)(cid:70)(cid:75)(cid:3)(cid:11)(cid:21)(cid:19)(cid:3)(cid:48)(cid:68)(cid:92)(cid:3)(cid:21)(cid:19)(cid:20)(cid:22)(cid:12)

The opinion draws attention to a particular modality of donation, and precisely to the possibility of 
having one’s body used, after death, both in study and research activities and in educational activi-
ties, such as anatomical dissection practices in training and refresher programmes for students 
and medical doctors. While observing the ethical value of the donation, the committee intends to 
emphasise the non-ethical acceptability of the provisions of Article 32 of Royal Decree No 1592 of 
31 August 1933, still in force, namely using in learning and study activities dead bodies of uniden-

The post-mortem donation of the body is based on a principle of solidarity towards others, pursued 
in this case through the promotion of study and research and indirectly through health protection. 
The key issue is that the donation should be the expression of a free and informed decision of the 
individual.

The opinion focuses on a particular aspect of healthcare organisation, the one concerning visiting 
polices (accompanying and family visits) in intensive care: an application, not always adequately 
considered,  of  the  principle  of  respect  of  individuals  within  healthcare  treatments  (Article  32, 
paragraph 2, ‘Cost’). This principle implies taking care of the patient, not just as an isolated indi-
vidual and as a mere body to be treated, but as a person with meaningful relationships from which 
he or she must not be forcibly separated, adding loneliness to the already serious disease condi-
tion, and in this way also achieving an improvement in the medical quality of treatments.

Numerous data suggest, in fact, that the promotion of access to IC for family members and visitors

of ‘open ICU’, bridging the gap with other countries in Europe and America that have long been 
successfully orientated in this direction. However, the opinion makes it clear that ‘open’ IC does 
not mean ‘no rules’ IC, and legislation that allows openings to be organised in order to protect 
the other values at stake is thus useful and necessary. The opinion therefore also highlights the 
problem of the standards of conduct which the visitors themselves must respect in order to ensure

to parents, the duty not to intervene in case of relentless trials, the role of ethics committees and

The committee believes that, in the case of adults, the twins’ will has to be considered in the same 
way as for every competent adult’s will, also with regard to the choice of undergoing experimental

the ethical review of biomedical research projects in Lithuania.

Composition of NEC
The institution consists of the administrative staff and two boards of experts, namely the Group 
of Experts on Biomedical Research and the Bioethics Council. The committee is managed by the 
director.

The mandate of the Group of Experts on Biomedical Research is to conduct a multidisciplinary 
review and to issue approvals for biomedical research projects, including clinical drug trials. The 
Group consists of nine members appointed for 1-year period by the director upon the authorisation 
of the Bioethics Council. It should, however, be noted that the approvals to conduct biomedical 
research projects which take place in a particular region of the country are issued by the regional 
research ethics committees.

The Bioethics Council deals with a broader scope of bioethical issues. It serves as a consulta-
tive body in the process of drafting normative documents related to the complex ethical issues in 
healthcare. It is also engaged in promoting public debate on bioethical issues as well as identifying 
the benchmarks for the future activities of the committee.

The members of the council are nominated by the minister of health for a 4-year period upon the 
recommendation of the director of the Lithuanian Bioethics Committee. The council is composed 
proportionally of representatives of the biomedical sciences and the humanities. Since 2011 it has 
consisted of 15 members, representing different backgrounds (biomedicine, law, psychology, phi-
losophy, theology, bioethics).

Reform of the Reproductive Medicine Act

The Austrian Reproductive Medicine Act prohibits the use of sperm from a donor for in vitro fertilisation 
and ovum donation in general. At the same time the act allows other assisted procreation techniques, in 
particular in vitro fertilisation with ova and sperm from the spouses or cohabitating partners themselves 
(homologous methods) and, in exceptional circumstances, donation of sperm when it is introduced into 
the reproductive organs of a woman.

This  situation  was  challenged  before  national  and  international  courts  on  grounds  of  discrimination 
and the violation of private and family life. In its ruling the European Court of Human Rights (ECHR) 
called on Austria to re-evaluate the Reproductive Medicine Act, which was promulgated in 1992 and has 
not been changed substantially since then, in view of new medical and societal developments, a process 
which was initiated by the Austrian Bioethics Commission.

In the early 21st century, parenthood and family no longer solely correspond to the traditional ideal of 
mother, father and child. An increasing number of families consist only of one parent and a child, and 
‘patchwork families’ of various combinations have been formed. These developments need to be trans-
(cid:79)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:72)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:87)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:191)(cid:72)(cid:79)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:73)(cid:3)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:83)(cid:85)(cid:82)(cid:71)(cid:88)(cid:70)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:89)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:80)(cid:72)(cid:71)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:72)(cid:17)

In its majority opinion on the reform of the Reproductive Medicine Act, the Austrian Bioethics Commis-
sion therefore:

(cid:135)(cid:3) calls for permitting sperm donations for in vitro treatments;

(cid:135)(cid:3) (cid:70)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:79)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:73)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:89)(cid:88)(cid:80)(cid:3)(cid:71)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:73)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:3)(cid:90)(cid:82)(cid:80)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:90)(cid:76)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:89)(cid:68)(cid:85)(cid:76)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:86)(cid:88)(cid:73)(cid:191)(cid:70)(cid:76)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:70)(cid:92)(cid:3)(cid:90)(cid:76)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:69)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:79)(cid:82)(cid:74)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:3)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:83)(cid:85)(cid:82)(cid:71)(cid:88)(cid:70)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:89)(cid:72)(cid:3)

phase;

(cid:135)(cid:3) calls for medically assisted reproduction outside marriage and consensual unions;

(cid:135)(cid:3)

recommends the administration of PGD for couples with a high risk of carrying a genetic predis-
position leading to a serious hereditary disease in their children and for the procurement of saviour 
siblings.

The dissenting opinion does not see a necessity of reform regarding the Reproductive Medicine Act and 
calls for more rigorous enforcement of the existing rules. It recommends enhancing international coop-

origin.

Research on persons without the capacity to consent — with special consideration of the concept 
of risk

The  right  of  self-determination  and  autonomy  of  the  patients  involved  in  a  clinical  trial  has  become 
a central element in the ethical assessment of clinical research projects. Hence the informed consent of 
patients is a prerequisite for their participation in research projects. Yet there are situations where patients 
are not or are no longer able to act autonomously (e.g. children, persons with dementia or patients in 
emergency settings) and therefore cannot give their consent to treatment or research.

The Austrian Bioethics Commission calls for the inclusion of persons without the capacity to consent 
subject to certain conditions (e.g. consultation of the relevant research ethics committee) and after thor-
ough risk assessment of the respective research projects, since the exclusion of certain groups of per-

computer  interfaces;  and  neural  stem-cell  therapies.  The  report  sets  out  an  ethical  framework  based 
around two fundamental considerations: the need for new approaches to treating serious disorders in the

(cid:55)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:83)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:70)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:70)(cid:79)(cid:88)(cid:71)(cid:72)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:85)(cid:82)(cid:83)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:74)(cid:88)(cid:79)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:76)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:191)(cid:72)(cid:79)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:80)(cid:88)(cid:86)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:85)(cid:82)(cid:80)(cid:82)(cid:87)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:81)(cid:82)(cid:89)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:90)(cid:75)(cid:76)(cid:79)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:85)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:76)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:86)-
ing the delivery of safe and effective neurotechnologies. The report received media coverage at the time 
of launch and the council has initiated a follow-up strategy for the major recommendations, including the 
following:

(cid:135)(cid:3) publicly accessible registers of data about the use of novel neurotechnologies should be established 

by professional bodies;

(cid:135)(cid:3) National Health Service bodies providing treatments using invasive neurotechnologies should be 
required to offer independent counselling, to provide an opportunity for patients and those close 
them to explore uncertainties and personal implications prior to choosing such treatments.

Donor conception: ethical aspects of information sharing

Parents of people conceived using donor eggs or sperm may or may not choose to tell them about their 
genetic origins. Some people feel strongly that information about a donor is essential for a donor-con-
ceived person, while others feel information about the use of donor sperm, eggs or embryos in fertility 
treatment is private to the parents and donors. Is this a private family matter, or are there wider public 
interests at stake? What kind of information might donor-conceived people and their parents need about 
their genetic origin? These are some of the questions addressed in the council’s report Donor conception: 
ethical aspects of information disclosure, published in April 2013.

The report which makes recommendations about the responsibilities of those involved in sharing infor-
mation about donor conception including donor-conceived people, parents, donors and health profession-
als, and it concluded that:

(cid:135)(cid:3) when, if and how to disclose should remain a private decision for families to take, but evidence 

suggests that it will usually be better for children to be told, and to be told at an early age;

(cid:135)(cid:3) measures that aim to support, encourage and empower those making decisions are preferable to 
measures that remove choice, thus the state has a ‘stewardship’ role in providing conditions that 
support and enable parents to make informed choices.

Children and clinical research

Clinical research involving children is essential to increase our understanding of childhood conditions 
and improve healthcare for children. Yet ethical problems abound — who should decide what research 
goes ahead? How much should children be involved in decisions about research participation? What are

to explore these and other questions arising from the involvement of children in research. The working

viduals also holds potentially far-reaching implications for privacy, interpersonal relationships and the 
relations  between  individuals  and  society.  This  working  party  is  exploring  the  ethical  issues  raised

Ethically speaking 18, September 2013

Ethics of security research — A recent law in Argentina — Helping justice

Comité Nacional de Ética en la Ciencia y en la Investigación de la República Argentina. Aída Kemelma-
jer de Carlucci

1. Purpose

The  purpose  of  this  report  is  to  briefly  provide  information  about  a  new  law  issued  in  Argentina  in 
July 2013. The aim of Law No 26879 is to facilitate the clarification of the facts subjected to a judicial 
investigation in criminal matters linked to offences against sexual integrity, in order to proceed with the 
identification of the people who are responsible for these crimes.

I will compare this Argentine law with two recent decisions, one from the Supreme Court of the United 
States (1) and the other from the ECHR (2).

The ethical problem is that, on the one hand, ‘it is beyond dispute that the fight against crime, and in 
particular against organised crime and terrorism, which is one of the challenges faced by today’s socie-
ties, depends to a great extent on the use of modern scientific techniques of investigation and identifica-
tion’ (3). The advent of DNA technology is one of the most significant scientific advancements of our era. 
The full potential for use of genetic markers in medicine and science is still being explored, but the utility 
of DNA identification in the criminal justice system is already undisputed, even if the primary purpose 
of these DNA searches is something other than simply discovering evidence of criminal wrongdoing.

On the other hand, ‘the taking and retention of a DNA sample is not a trivial matter and, absent a com-
pelling public interest, would inherently constitute a grave intrusion on the subject’s right to personal and 
informational privacy’ (4).

Therefore, the ECHR observes that the protection afforded by Article 8 of the European Convention on 
Human Rights would be unacceptably weakened if the use of modern scientific techniques in the crimi-
nal justice system were allowed at any cost and without carefully balancing the potential benefits of the 
extensive use of such techniques against important private‑life interests. As a rule, the taking of cellular 
material and its retention as well as the determination and retention of DNA profiles extracted from cel-
lular samples constitute an interference with the right to respect for private life within the meaning of 
Article 8(1) of the convention. Such interference will be in breach of Article 8 of the convention unless 
it can be justified (a) as being in accordance with the law’, (b) as pursuing one or more of the legitimate 
aims listed therein and (c) as being necessary in a democratic society in order to achieve the aim or aims 
concerned.

This is the reason why domestic law must afford appropriate safeguards to prevent any use of personal 
data that may be inconsistent with the guarantees of Article 8. The domestic law should notably ensure: 

(1)  Maryland v. King, 3.6.2013. Justice Scalia, with whom Justice Ginsburg, Justice Sotomayor and Justice Kagan join, dis-

senting.

(2)  Peruzzo and Martens v. Germany, 4.6.2013, Applications Nos 7841/08 and 57900/12.
(3)  TEHR, S. and Marper v. United Kingdom, Applications Nos  30562/04 and 30566/04, 4 December 2008. The first appli-
cant, Mr S., was arrested on 19 January 2001 at the age of 11 and charged with attempted robbery. His fingerprints and 
DNA samples were taken. He was acquitted on 14 June 2001. The second applicant, Mr Michael Marper, was arrested 
on 13 March 2001 and charged with harassment of his partner. His fingerprints and DNA samples were taken. Before 
a pre‑trial review took place, he and his partner had reconciled, and the charge was not pressed. On 11 June 2001, the 
Crown Prosecution Service served a notice of discontinuance on the applicant’s solicitors, and on 14 June 2001 the case 
was formally discontinued.

(4)  Supreme Court of Canada, R. v. R.C. [2005] 3 SCR 99, 2005 SCC 61. With the same idea, dissenting opinion of the US 
Supreme Court: ‘Solving unsolved crimes is a noble objective, but it occupies a lower place in the American pantheon of 
noble objectives than the protection of our people from suspicion less law‑enforcement searches. The Fourth Amendment 
must prevail’.

from misuse and abuse. These considerations are especially valid as regards the protection of special 
categories of more sensitive data, and more particularly of DNA information, which contains the person’s 
genetic make-up and is of great importance to both the person concerned and his or her family.

A margin of appreciation must be left to the competent national authorities. The breadth of this margin 
varies and depends on a number of factors, including the nature of the convention right in issue, its 
importance for the individual, the nature of the interference and the object pursued by the interference. 
The margin will tend to be narrower where the right at stake is crucial to the individual’s effective enjoy-
ment of intimate or key rights.

2. The importance of DNA — Differences with other technical instruments

It is a well-recognised aspect of criminal conduct that the perpetrator will take unusual steps to conceal 
not only his conduct, but also his identity. Disguises used while committing a crime may be supple-
mented or replaced by changed names, and even changed physical features. The judgment in Maryland 
v. King states that ‘people detained for minor offences can turn out to be the most devious and dangerous

3. General situation in Europe

At least 20 Council of Europe (CoE) member countries make provision for the taking of DNA infor-
mation and its storage on national databases or in other forms (Austria, Belgium, the Czech Republic, 
Denmark, Estonia, Finland, France, Germany, Greece, Hungary, Ireland, Italy, Latvia, Luxembourg, the 
Netherlands, Norway, Poland, Spain, Sweden and Switzerland). This number is steadily increasing.

In most of these states (including Austria, Belgium, Finland, France, Germany, Hungary, Ireland, Italy, 
Luxembourg, the Netherlands, Norway, Poland, Spain and Sweden), the taking of DNA information in

Recommendation No R (92) 1 of the CoE Committee of Ministers on the use of analysis of deoxyribo-
nucleic acid (DNA) within the framework of the criminal justice system (adopted on 10 February 1992) 
states as follows: ‘3. Use of samples and information derived therefrom. Samples collected for DNA 
analysis and the information derived from such analysis for the purpose of the investigation and prosecu-
tion of criminal offences must not be used for other purposes’.

In the same sense, the Argentine law declares ‘The data in the registry will be regarded as sensitive and 
restricted, so it only will be provided to members of the public prosecutor, judges and courts around the 
country as part of a cause in which offences against sexual integrity are being investigated … Within 
the framework of this law, the use of samples of deoxyribonucleic acid (DNA) is banned for any purpose

6. How to take samples

The Argentine law does not mention the way DNA must be collected. The Maryland DNA Collection 
Act mentioned a common procedure known as a ‘buccal swab’. ‘Buccal cell collection involves wiping 
(cid:68)(cid:3)(cid:86)(cid:80)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:79)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:76)(cid:72)(cid:70)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:73)(cid:3)(cid:191)(cid:79)(cid:87)(cid:72)(cid:85)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:68)(cid:83)(cid:72)(cid:85)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:3)(cid:70)(cid:82)(cid:87)(cid:87)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:86)(cid:90)(cid:68)(cid:69)(cid:3)(cid:86)(cid:76)(cid:80)(cid:76)(cid:79)(cid:68)(cid:85)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:3)(cid:52)(cid:16)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:83)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:74)(cid:68)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:86)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:86)(cid:76)(cid:71)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:70)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:72)(cid:78)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:73)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:76)(cid:89)(cid:76)(cid:71)(cid:88)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:182)(cid:86)(cid:3)
mouth to collect some skin cells.’ The procedure is quick and painless. The swab touches inside an arres-
tee’s mouth, but it requires no ‘surgical intrusion beneath the skin’, and it poses no ‘threat to the health 
or safety’ of arrestees. ‘A buccal swab is a far more gentle process than a venipuncture to draw blood.’ It 
involves but a light touch on the inside of the cheek; and although it can be deemed a search within the 
body of the arrestee, it requires no ‘surgical intrusions beneath the skin’.

suspected. Moreover, there exist only limited possibilities for an acquitted individual to have the data 
removed from the national database or the materials destroyed; in particular, there is no provision for 
(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:72)(cid:83)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:89)(cid:76)(cid:72)(cid:90)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:73)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:77)(cid:88)(cid:86)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:191)(cid:70)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:73)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:87)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:70)(cid:70)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:71)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:74)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:71)(cid:72)(cid:191)(cid:81)(cid:72)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:70)(cid:85)(cid:76)(cid:87)(cid:72)(cid:85)(cid:76)(cid:68)(cid:15)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:70)(cid:79)(cid:88)(cid:71)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:74)(cid:3)(cid:86)(cid:88)(cid:70)(cid:75)(cid:3)(cid:73)(cid:68)(cid:70)-
tors as the seriousness of the offence, previous arrests, the strength of the suspicion against the person 
or any other special circumstances. The retention of cellular samples is particularly intrusive given the 
wealth of genetic and health information contained therein. Of particular concern in the present context 
is the risk of stigmatisation, stemming from the fact that persons who have not been convicted of any 
offence and are entitled to the presumption of innocence are treated in the same way as convicted per-
sons. In this respect, the ECHR must bear in mind that the right of every person under the convention to 
be presumed innocent includes the general rule that no suspicion regarding an accused’s innocence may 
be voiced after his acquittal. The ECHR further considers that the retention of unconvicted persons’ data 
may be especially harmful in the case of minors, given their special situation and the importance of their 
development and integration in society.

According to the Argentine law, the information in the registry will be deleted only one hundred (100) 
years after the initiation of the cause in which its incorporation was arranged.

9. Comparative conclusions

According to the general rules issued from the American and European decisions taken into account for 
this report I conclude the following.

(cid:135)(cid:3)

In general, the Argentine law is not against human rights.

(cid:135)(cid:3) However, it has some weak points:

(cid:135)(cid:3)

(cid:135)(cid:3)

the length of storage is too long; a hundred years is more than a normal person could live;

the absolute silence about age leaves children and adolescents in a bad situation, not compatible 
with the Convention on the Rights of the Child;

(cid:135)(cid:3) (cid:72)(cid:89)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:73)(cid:3)(cid:70)(cid:85)(cid:76)(cid:80)(cid:72)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:74)(cid:68)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:86)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:86)(cid:72)(cid:91)(cid:88)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:87)(cid:72)(cid:74)(cid:85)(cid:76)(cid:87)(cid:92)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:71)(cid:76)(cid:73)(cid:191)(cid:70)(cid:88)(cid:79)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:85)(cid:82)(cid:89)(cid:72)(cid:15)(cid:3)(cid:90)(cid:82)(cid:80)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:89)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:80)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:81)(cid:72)(cid:72)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:86)(cid:83)(cid:72)(cid:70)(cid:76)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:3)
state protection according to the Committee on the Elimination of Discrimination against Women, 
and there is no reason to distinguish between crimes against sexual integrity and other crimes

The ethics of research security and of security research: a few words about Brazil

Iara Coelho Zito Guerriero (7)

One point that stands out when referring to the ethics of security research is cybernetic security. Brazil 
has been discussing it and taking action. Another important element is the improvement of informa-
tion technologies that have made the storage of huge amounts of personal data possible, allowing their 
analysis. Such analyses should be publically available. The possibility of linkages increases the possibil-
ity of identifying individuals, even when the database has previously been anonymised. In this article, 
I present the Brazilian commitment to cybernetic security and its actions; the legal duty to guarantee

Cybernetic security

The Brazilian government considers cybersecurity to be a strategic mandate of the state.

Access to public information

Based on the Brazilian constitution, all information produced or held by the state is of a public nature 
and should be publicly available. Information produced by the state includes the epidemiological data-
bases. Therefore, access to public information is a citizen’s right in Brazil.

Therefore, the state has to protect privacy and allow access to public information. In practical terms, 
there are situations in which it may be challenging to meet these two duties, creating ethical dilemmas. 
Especially if we move to the dual use of the data collected by health services, for the purpose of public 
health, it then becomes necessary to inform researchers. This frequently happens and involves different 
interests. It raises at least two ethical questions, as follows.

(cid:135)(cid:3) Should these data be used for purposes other than those to which the person consented? The gold

research.

In biorepositories, each new research project has to receive ethical approval and each person has to sign 
a new consent form.

In biobanks, when people allow the storage of their material they choose whether or not they want to sign 
a new consent form for each research project.

The free and informed consent, in both situations, should provide information about what kind of results 
can be obtained from their material; each people has the right to know these results and researchers 
should offer genetic counselling, if applicable.

for example, if it was gathered in a research context this biological material cannot be used for purposes

Final consideration

Nowadays, with more and more new technologies, such as IT or genetic tests, to quote two, it is challeng-
(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:74)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:80)(cid:72)(cid:72)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:72)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:79)(cid:72)(cid:74)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:3)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:84)(cid:88)(cid:76)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:80)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:87)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:79)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:74)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:85)(cid:82)(cid:87)(cid:72)(cid:70)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:73)(cid:3)(cid:70)(cid:76)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:93)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:86)(cid:182)(cid:3)(cid:70)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:191)(cid:71)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:76)(cid:87)(cid:92)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:3)
to ensure that society is kept secure. Thinking about health security, the dual use of data and biological 
material can be necessary in the interests of public health.

However, where are the limits? When can the duty to keep information private be set aside in the name 
of the security of society? These ethical dilemmas deserve in-depth discussions at national and interna-
tional level.

Year of establishment of NEC 
2001

Remit of NEC
The Canadian Institutes of Health Research (CIHR), Canada’s premier federal agency for health 
research, has a legislative mandate in ethics. The ethics mandate consists of promoting, assisting 
and undertaking health research that meets the highest standards of ethics; fostering discussion of 
ethical issues and application of ethical principles to health research; monitoring, analysing and 
evaluating ethical issues pertaining to health or health research; and encouraging interdiscipli-
nary, innovative and integrative research on ethical issues pertaining to health.

CHINA

NEC name: Ethics Committee — Ministry of Health — China

1.

2.

Year of establishment of NEC 
2000

Remit of NEC
1.  Address  ethical  issues  in  healthcare  and  biomedical  research,  provide  counselling  for  deci-
sion-making, give ethical assessment to those decisions which have already been in practice or are 
going to be effective and provide opinions to revise and improve them.

2. Address ethical issues emerging in biomedical research, public health and the application of 
new technologies, and timely provide recommendations.

3. Provide guidance, information and training to local and institutional committees, and espe-
cially play a guiding role in promoting the implementation of international ethical guidelines to 
take into account practical regional and national situations.

4. Review the protocols of grand programmes or major programmes of international cooperation.

3.

MEXICO

NEC name: Comisión Nacional de Bioética (National Bioethics Commission)

1.

2.

3.

4.

5.

6.

Year of establishment of NEC
The National Bioethics Commission of México (NBC) was established in 1992. However, it 
started a new era through the presidential decree issued in September 2005 that created it as 
a deconcentrated body, with technical and operative autonomy.

Remit of NEC
The comprehensive mission of the NBC is to promote a bioethics culture in Mexico, by a lay 
analysis,  plural  and  inclusive  of  bioethical  dilemmas,  based  on  respect  for  the  rights  of  the 
human being, the protection of living beings and preservation of the environment.

Composition of NEC
The council:

President of the Council (4-year mandate).

Counsellors: six honorary (designated for 2-year mandate by the Secretary of Health). The coun-
cil tends to be multidisciplinary, plural and gender balanced (secular).

Executive director.

Academic and operational units and the Bioethical Knowledge Centre (Cecobe).

List of recent NEC opinions
Specialised reviews on legislative proposals analysed by Conbioetica:

(cid:135)(cid:3) assisted human reproduction and gestational surrogacy,

(cid:135)(cid:3) conscientious objection,

(cid:135)(cid:3) comprehensive care at the end of life.

List of future topics covered by NEC opinions
The next issues generating interest and attention are as follows:

Institutional achievements of the National Bioethics Commission in Mexico

Manuel H. Ruiz de Chávez, MD, MSC

The National Bioethics Commission (Conbioética), established in 1992, became a deconcentrated agency 
of the Federal Secretariat of Health in 2005 through a presidential decree, which gave it technical and 
(cid:82)(cid:83)(cid:72)(cid:85)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:88)(cid:87)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:82)(cid:80)(cid:92)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:86)(cid:83)(cid:72)(cid:70)(cid:76)(cid:191)(cid:70)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:68)(cid:86)(cid:78)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:73)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:85)(cid:82)(cid:80)(cid:82)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:74)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:3)(cid:69)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:72)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:3)(cid:70)(cid:88)(cid:79)(cid:87)(cid:88)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:48)(cid:72)(cid:91)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:85)(cid:82)(cid:88)(cid:74)(cid:75)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:79)(cid:88)(cid:85)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:15)(cid:3)
secular and inclusive perspective, based on the respect for human rights and the protection of all living 
beings and the environment.

There are many important moments in the history of this institution, as it has been a long struggle to 
(cid:82)(cid:83)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:81)(cid:72)(cid:90)(cid:3)(cid:86)(cid:83)(cid:68)(cid:70)(cid:72)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:191)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:73)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:71)(cid:72)(cid:89)(cid:72)(cid:79)(cid:82)(cid:83)(cid:80)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:73)(cid:3)(cid:69)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:72)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:86)(cid:15)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:86)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:69)(cid:72)(cid:70)(cid:82)(cid:80)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:3)(cid:70)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:86)(cid:82)(cid:79)(cid:76)(cid:71)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:72)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:73)(cid:72)(cid:85)-
(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:70)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:82)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:81)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:90)(cid:76)(cid:71)(cid:72)(cid:15)(cid:3)(cid:69)(cid:88)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:86)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:87)(cid:72)(cid:85)(cid:81)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:3)(cid:79)(cid:72)(cid:89)(cid:72)(cid:79)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:76)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:191)(cid:72)(cid:79)(cid:71)(cid:17)(cid:3)(cid:36)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:3)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:86)(cid:88)(cid:79)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:73)(cid:3)(cid:80)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:92)(cid:3)(cid:72)(cid:73)(cid:73)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:87)(cid:86)(cid:15)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:38)(cid:82)(cid:81)-
bioética acquired a new academic seat in the National Academy of Medicine of Mexico in 2013. Also, 
through the Centre of Bioethical Knowledge (Cecobe), the Conbioética has developed more adequate 
spaces to interact with members of the public that are interested in bioethics, with a proper space for 
public consultation and equipment for virtual and long-distance communication. The library offers free 
information services to the general population and also students, academics and researchers in bioethics, 
or members of bioethics and ethics committees, through printed and digital publications, and diverse 
databases and networks with the latest information available. This is crucial since bioethics in Mexico 
have become now attainable for everybody that is interested through updated high-quality information, 
avant-garde facilities, proper public spaces and technology suited especially to promote bioethical cul-
ture among society.

Also, the Conbioética has just started a new programme called ‘Bioethics for youth’, which is aimed at 
young students, with the purpose of creating networks of young people around bioethics, and generat-
ing spaces for dialogue and interaction between the institution and students, but also between students 
themselves,  in  order  to  interest  them  in  bioethics  and  let  them  approach  these  topics  with  their  own 
perspective with a proper orientation, as it is they who will make decisions on bioethical issues, either 
professionally or personally, on a daily basis, as we all do. The task is to motivate them through different 
activities that involve them in bioethics, which can be encompassed in three main lines of action: media; 
physical and artistic activities; and academic activities. The Conbioética has already started to contact 
students and learn their interests in order to develop the content of the activities, and has been present in 
several activities with students of the National Autonomous University of Mexico (UNAM), and also in 
some forums talking with students about bioethics.

Also, the Conbioética has made a commitment to reinforce the institutionalisation of bioethics in Mex-
ico, meaning state bioethics commissions, hospital bioethics committees (HBCs) and research ethics 

66 

Ethically speaking 18, September 2013

committees (RECs). The Conbioética supported the reformation of the General Health Act in order to 
establish as mandatory the existence of HBCs and RECs in every healthcare and research institution in 
Mexico, and it issued regulations regarding the registration and integration of these committees which 
have been published through an agreement from the secretary of health, strengthening its normative 
function.

This  step  also  means  that  society  will  require  more  bioethicists  that  are  capable  of  operating  on  an 
institutional level, and therefore learning bioethics — theory and practice — has become a big public 
demand. Therefore, the Conbioética has encouraged and given orientation to several institutions that are 
interested or that already have programmes or courses on bioethics, and is currently working on devel-
oping a basic course for members of research ethics committees and hospital bioethics committees with 
the university programme on bioethics of the UNAM. Likewise, the Conbioética collaborated with the 
Autonomous Technological Institute of Mexico (ITAM) in the development of a bioethics and law course 
that took place during August 2013, among other courses and seminars that have been organised and in 
which the Conbioética has taken part in many academic institutions throughout the country.

There is a lot of ground to be covered, and the Conbioética is aware that in order to develop bioethics 
everybody must participate, but they also need leadership and orientation. That is why the Conbioética 
(cid:75)(cid:68)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:88)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:72)(cid:85)(cid:87)(cid:68)(cid:78)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:76)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:68)(cid:86)(cid:78)(cid:15)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:3)(cid:81)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:86)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:87)(cid:72)(cid:85)(cid:81)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:82)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:73)(cid:3)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:73)(cid:72)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:70)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:191)(cid:72)(cid:79)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:73)(cid:3)(cid:69)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:72)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:86)(cid:15)(cid:3)
with the purpose of giving our society enough elements to participate, under democratic standards, in 
this public, plural, secular, interdisciplinary dialogue we call bioethics, which is open to everybody in 
order to make decisions that have included different perspectives and that actually respond not only to 
our needs but to our obligation of responsibility towards other living beings, the environment and future 
generations.

3. Have at least nine members, with 60 % constituting a quorum.

(cid:24)(cid:17)(cid:3)(cid:44)(cid:81)(cid:70)(cid:79)(cid:88)(cid:71)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:79)(cid:72)(cid:68)(cid:86)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:90)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:79)(cid:68)(cid:92)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:72)(cid:85)(cid:86)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:86)(cid:3)(cid:90)(cid:75)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:75)(cid:68)(cid:89)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:81)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:73)(cid:191)(cid:79)(cid:76)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:86)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:87)(cid:88)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:15)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:81)(cid:82)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:70)(cid:88)(cid:85)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:87)(cid:79)(cid:92)(cid:3)
(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:89)(cid:82)(cid:79)(cid:89)(cid:72)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:80)(cid:72)(cid:71)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:15)(cid:3)(cid:86)(cid:70)(cid:76)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:191)(cid:70)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:3)(cid:79)(cid:72)(cid:74)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:3)(cid:90)(cid:82)(cid:85)(cid:78)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:68)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:85)(cid:72)(cid:73)(cid:72)(cid:85)(cid:68)(cid:69)(cid:79)(cid:92)(cid:3)(cid:73)(cid:85)(cid:82)(cid:80)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:70)(cid:82)(cid:80)(cid:80)(cid:88)(cid:81)(cid:76)(cid:87)(cid:92)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:90)(cid:75)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:75)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)
research is to take place.

6. Include at least one member with knowledge of, and current experience in, areas of research 
that are likely to be regularly considered by the ethics committee.

7. Include at least one member with knowledge of, and current experience in, the professional 
care, counselling or treatment of people. Such a member might be, for example, be a medical prac-
titioner, psychologist, social worker or nurse.

8. Include at least one member who has professional training in both qualitative and quantitative 
research methodologies.

9. Include at least one member who is legally trained.

10. The institution or organisation must ensure that the membership is equipped to address all 
relevant considerations arising from the categories of research likely to be submitted to it.

Remit of NEC
The National Advisory Commission on Biomedical Ethics is an extra-parliamentary body. It was 
(cid:72)(cid:86)(cid:87)(cid:68)(cid:69)(cid:79)(cid:76)(cid:86)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:69)(cid:92)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:41)(cid:72)(cid:71)(cid:72)(cid:85)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:3)(cid:38)(cid:82)(cid:88)(cid:81)(cid:70)(cid:76)(cid:79)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:22)(cid:3)(cid:45)(cid:88)(cid:79)(cid:92)(cid:3)(cid:21)(cid:19)(cid:19)(cid:20)(cid:17)

The commission monitors the development of the sciences relating to human health and disease,

Swiss National Advisory Commission on Biomedical Ethics

Legal basis and mandate

In  the  debate  on  the  Federal  Act  on  Medically  Assisted  Reproduction  (Reproductive  Medicine  Act, 
FMedG), there was agreement in both chambers of the Swiss parliament that it was important — given 
the ever-increasing relevance of the issues — to establish a permanent body concerned with biomedical 
ethics. Under Article 28 of the act, approved by parliament on 18 December 1998, a ‘national ethics com-
mission’ was to be set up as an independent, extra-parliamentary deliberative body.

Decision-making process regarding medical treatment in end-of-life situations

The Committee on Bioethics (DH-BIO) has been continuing its work on the decision-making process 
regarding medical treatment in end-of-life situations. In this context, a preliminary draft guide aimed 
at facilitating the implementation of the principles laid down in the Convention on Human Rights and 
Biomedicine, and in particular Article 9 concerning previously expressed wishes, was examined by the 
DH-BIO at its December plenary meeting. It was agreed to make it public for consultation as a work-

The  exhibition  takes  the  viewer  on  a  journey  through  the  history  of  health  ethics  and  illustrates  the 
diversity of the ethical issues in medicine, public health and health research today. The Global Health 
Ethics Unit hopes that the exhibition will answer the many questions one may have on health ethics, and 
stimulate some more to foster debate.

Ethics of tuberculosis prevention, care and control

Prevention, care and control of tuberculosis (TB) do not only raise technical and operational challenges, 
but also important ethical and human rights issues that TB programmes must address. These have been 
(cid:75)(cid:76)(cid:74)(cid:75)(cid:79)(cid:76)(cid:74)(cid:75)(cid:87)(cid:72)(cid:71)(cid:3)(cid:69)(cid:92)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:72)(cid:80)(cid:72)(cid:85)(cid:74)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:70)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:73)(cid:3)(cid:48)(cid:39)(cid:53)(cid:18)(cid:59)(cid:39)(cid:53)(cid:16)(cid:55)(cid:37)(cid:17)

After the publication of the WHO’s Guidance on Ethics of tuberculosis prevention, care and control 
((cid:75)(cid:87)(cid:87)(cid:83)(cid:29)(cid:18)(cid:18)(cid:90)(cid:75)(cid:84)(cid:79)(cid:76)(cid:69)(cid:71)(cid:82)(cid:70)(cid:17)(cid:90)(cid:75)(cid:82)(cid:17)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:87)(cid:18)(cid:83)(cid:88)(cid:69)(cid:79)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:86)(cid:18)(cid:21)(cid:19)(cid:20)(cid:19)(cid:18)(cid:28)(cid:26)(cid:27)(cid:28)(cid:21)(cid:23)(cid:20)(cid:24)(cid:19)(cid:19)(cid:24)(cid:22)(cid:20)(cid:66)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:74)(cid:17)(cid:83)(cid:71)(cid:73))  at  the  end  of  2010,  a  series  of 
implementation activities have been organised at regional and country level.

In November 2012, the WHO carried out a workshop on the ethics of TB in Kuala Lumpur, Malay-
sia, with 50 participants from Azerbaijan, Belgium, India, Kazakhstan, Kyrgyzstan, Malaysia, Nepal, 
Nigeria, Pakistan, the Philippines, the United States Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC), 
United States Agency for International Development (USAID) and Uzbekistan.

Science and Technology (COST) action on ethics in disasters network, sponsored by the European Com-

International Clinical Trial Registry Platform

Following the resolution adopted by the World Health Assembly in 2005, the International Clinical Trial 
Registry Platform (ICTRP) was established to promote transparency in health research activities. The 
platform is a ‘one-stop shop’ that provides information on all trials registered by any WHO primary reg-
istry. The standards adopted by the WHO primary registries contribute to harmonise procedures and to 
ensure the quality of data collection. In March of 2012 the EU Clinical Trials Register (EU-CTR) became 

Ethically speaking 18, September 2013 

87

a data provider to the ICTRP Search Portal, and in 2013 Thailand was approved as a primary registry 
for the ICTRP, thus bringing the total number of national primary registries to 15. The ICTRP database 
now contains more than 245 000 records of clinical trials. More information is available on the Internet 
((cid:75)(cid:87)(cid:87)(cid:83)(cid:29)(cid:18)(cid:18)(cid:90)(cid:90)(cid:90)(cid:17)(cid:90)(cid:75)(cid:82)(cid:17)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:87)(cid:18)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:87)(cid:85)(cid:83)(cid:18)(cid:72)(cid:81)(cid:18)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:71)(cid:72)(cid:91)(cid:17)(cid:75)(cid:87)(cid:80)(cid:79)).

Online course on research ethics

GHE is working with the University of Oxford to adapt the online research ethics course developed for 
WHO staff members for a global audience. The launch of the adapted online course is planned before the 
end of 2013. The venue of the launch is still under discussion.

in low- and middle-income countries, are to be designated in the near future.

(b) 10th Global Summit of National Ethics Committees (NECs)

The 10th(cid:3)(cid:86)(cid:88)(cid:80)(cid:80)(cid:76)(cid:87)(cid:3)(cid:90)(cid:76)(cid:79)(cid:79)(cid:3)(cid:87)(cid:68)(cid:78)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:83)(cid:79)(cid:68)(cid:70)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:48)(cid:72)(cid:91)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:82)(cid:3)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:3)(cid:21)(cid:21)(cid:177)(cid:21)(cid:23)(cid:3)(cid:45)(cid:88)(cid:81)(cid:72)(cid:3)(cid:21)(cid:19)(cid:20)(cid:23)(cid:17)

The Global Summit of NECs is an initiative that was taken by NECs in 1996. The Global Health Ethics 
Unit at the WHO serves as the permanent secretariat of the summit, and as such it provides technical 
support to working groups of NECs established to address priority ethical issues and works in close col-

tion to the summit, and to establish the working groups and decide on the topics to be considered at the 
summit. A database of NECs was developed to provide access to opinions published by NECs (see (cid:75)(cid:87)(cid:87)(cid:83)(cid:29)(cid:18)(cid:18)
(cid:68)(cid:83)(cid:83)(cid:86)(cid:17)(cid:90)(cid:75)(cid:82)(cid:17)(cid:76)(cid:81)(cid:87)(cid:18)(cid:72)(cid:87)(cid:75)(cid:76)(cid:70)(cid:86)(cid:18)(cid:81)(cid:68)(cid:87)(cid:76)(cid:82)(cid:81)(cid:68)(cid:79)(cid:70)(cid:82)(cid:80)(cid:80)(cid:76)(cid:87)(cid:87)(cid:72)(cid:72)(cid:86)); this tool should facilitate exchange between NECs on issues of
